    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer15921364136825058742">Impish, foul-mouthed Zazie arrives in Paris from the country to stay with her uncle Gabriel. All she really wants to do is ride the metro, but finding it shut because of a strike, Zazie looks for other means of amusement and is soon caught up in a comic adventure that becomes wilder and more manic by the minute. <br/><br/>In 1960 Queneau's cult classic was made into a hugely success</span>
  <span id="freeText15921364136825058742" style="display:none">Impish, foul-mouthed Zazie arrives in Paris from the country to stay with her uncle Gabriel. All she really wants to do is ride the metro, but finding it shut because of a strike, Zazie looks for other means of amusement and is soon caught up in a comic adventure that becomes wilder and more manic by the minute. <br /><br />In 1960 Queneau's cult classic was made into a hugely successful film by Louis Malle. Packed full of word play and phonetic games, 'Zazie in the Metro' remains as stylish and witty today as it did back then.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1331713007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2815881204575949442"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492964221i/22571468._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>“Being or nothing, that is the question. Ascending, descending, coming, going, a man does so much that in the end he disappears.”<br/>― Raymond Queneau, <i>Zazie in the Metro</i> <br/><br/>A Raymond Queneau funny-bone tickler, a witty zazissle through Paris with Uncle Gabriel hosting visiting Zazie, the ever oh so very loveable little wisecracker who oh so very much wishes to ride the city’s Metro. And I was ever on the qt for how many of RQ’s 99 exercises in style turn up displaying their linguistic proboscis in thi</span>
  <span id="freeText2815881204575949442" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492964221i/22571468._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>“Being or nothing, that is the question. Ascending, descending, coming, going, a man does so much that in the end he disappears.”<br>― Raymond Queneau, <i>Zazie in the Metro</i> <br><br>A Raymond Queneau funny-bone tickler, a witty zazissle through Paris with Uncle Gabriel hosting visiting Zazie, the ever oh so very loveable little wisecracker who oh so very much wishes to ride the city’s Metro. And I was ever on the qt for how many of RQ’s 99 exercises in style turn up displaying their linguistic proboscis in this whimsical 150 page chocolate croissant. Oodles and oodles as per this snatch of dialogue in the opening pages: <br><br>Not displeased with his turn of phrase, the little chap wasn’t. Only the great hulk didn’t let up, it leant over him and uttered this monophasic pentasyllable:<br>‘Wottusaidjustnow.’<br>The little chap began to get apprehensive. Now was his time, now was the moment to forge some sort of verbal buckler. The first that came into his head was an alexandrine:<br>‘And anyway who said that you could call me tu?’<br>‘Yellow-belly,’ retorted Gabriel with simplicity.’<br><br>That's alexandrine (like many readers, I have to look up such words), as in a line of poetic meter comprising 12 syllables. Anyway, this little book, is sooo much fun. That’s so much fun as in novel as literary polyphony, one melodic line being the light, lyrical, oh-so-very-much-like a French film storyline of Zazie’s romp; the second melodic line as in the ever present fun and games with all phases of language: spelling, slang, colloquialisms, malapropisms, grammar, syntax, run-ons and run-overs and many of those 99 fanciful exercises in style. One more for instance to service up a la fin:<br><br>She dares not articulate the disyllabic and anglo-saxon word which would mean what she means. It’s the chap who pronounces it.<br>‘You wouldn’t by any chance have a pair of blewgenes for the little girl? He asks the middleman. ‘That is what you’d like?’<br>‘Oh yess,’ yespers Zazie. <br><br>Tally-ho with Raymond Queneau!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492964221i/22571469._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Some of the letters that make their appearance in this Raymond Queneau novel novel</span>
  <a data-text-id="2815881204575949442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1346757496" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1346757496" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1346757496?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070361038" href="/book/show/876549.Zazie_dans_le_m_tro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=reread">reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=oulipo-word-puzzles">oulipo-word-puzzles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=review-may-contain-comic-content">review-may-contain-comic-content</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=read-in-french">read-in-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=queneau">queneau</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1346757496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13197369959446189798">I’m going to hazard a crazy guess as to what this book is really about—besides the immediately recognizable themes which others have analyzed and canonized, themes such as the bizzareries Zazie encounters in the metropolis and the dazzling use of slang in the narrative.<br/><br/>But before I hazard my crazy guess, you’ll have to be patient with me while I do a little hypothesizing, while I attempt to puzzle it all out. Because I really am trying to get to the bottom of Queneau’s intention here. I’m attemp</span>
  <span id="freeText13197369959446189798" style="display:none">I’m going to hazard a crazy guess as to what this book is really about—besides the immediately recognizable themes which others have analyzed and canonized, themes such as the bizzareries Zazie encounters in the metropolis and the dazzling use of slang in the narrative.<br /><br />But before I hazard my crazy guess, you’ll have to be patient with me while I do a little hypothesizing, while I attempt to puzzle it all out. Because I really am trying to get to the bottom of Queneau’s intention here. I’m attempting to look behind the pizzazz of the wordplay, to fully fathom the fundaments of this underground story (and the subversion of language and narrative which characterizes the book, of course).<br /><br /><i>Tu causes, tu causes, c’est tout ce que tu sais faire! <br />Chatter, chatter, it's all you know how to do!</i><br /><br />You might, at this point, be getting impatient, and inclined to repeat Laverdure's words (Laverdure is a parrot who parrots a perfect parody of both himself and his owner at every turn of the narrative). But I’m not like them, I’m not such a quiz. I really do have something to say if I could only cough it up, spew it out, as it were (ok, sorry for being such a scuzz). But my crazy thesis about this book is buried deep, it really is, deeper than the maze of underground passages through which softly spoken Marceline zigzags in the last pages (and little wonder Marceline speaks softly with a name like that! (and little wonder Marceline is who Marceline is in the first place! (and please excuse all this overzealous parenthesizing))).<br /><br /><i>Tu causes, tu causes, c’est tout ce que tu sais faire!<br />Chatter, chatter, it's all you know how to do!</i><br /><br />I hear your frustration. Yess, yess, yess (more soft sounds just to say I’m no gongoozler. No, I’m very busy tunnelling down to reach my crazy theory, and the last pages are not irrelevant: ‘Last’, that whizz of a word, is actually very pertinent to my crazy thesis, viz, the phrase ‘the last will be first’).<br /><br /><i>Tu causes, tu causes, c’est tout ce que tu sais faire!<br />Chatter, chatter, it's all you know how to do!</i><br /><br />Ok, I realise I’m in danger of touching <i>hyperboliquement le cercle vicieux de la parabole*</i> so don’t get frazzled, I’m going to get straight to the point, no more zig-zagging, no more buzzing about like a zesty scherzo, no more spritzing myself into a dizzy tizzy: Queneau’s subversion of the language of narrative, his use of slang, all those run-on words and phonetic spellings, <i>par egzemple: egzagèrer, vouzêtes, etc</i> is his attempt to undermine the established order, to turn everything on its head, to put what comes normally at the bottom on the top. <br /><br />You knew that all along? <br />You knew that the clue was in the title? <br />You knew that it was really about celebrating the last letter of the alphabet? <br />The letter Z?<br /><br /><i>Mon cul!**</i><br /><br />.......................................................................<br /><br />*a marvelous Queneau phrase that mixes rhetoric and maths and more or less accuses me, and some of the characters, of talking rubbish in a way that is both shallow and deep at the same time - not an easy task, I swear!<br />**<i>Mon cul</i> is Zazie’s favourite swear word. It translates as ‘My arse!’ if you're Irish, ‘My ass!’ if you’re American, and if you're English...'My bottom'.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13197369959446189798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2170440430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9193063761938763263">Another one which I read because of a book group selection, this time in the <b>Forgotten Classics...</b> group. It is a book that is impossible to distance completely from the film it inspired, a wild playful comic adventure set in a Paris netherworld that clearly owes much to Joyce. <br/><br/>The language is full of invention, with hybrid words and all manner of allusions and wordplay - this must have made the translator's job very difficult and although Barbara Wright handled this task admirably and creativel</span>
  <span id="freeText9193063761938763263" style="display:none">Another one which I read because of a book group selection, this time in the <b>Forgotten Classics...</b> group. It is a book that is impossible to distance completely from the film it inspired, a wild playful comic adventure set in a Paris netherworld that clearly owes much to Joyce. <br /><br />The language is full of invention, with hybrid words and all manner of allusions and wordplay - this must have made the translator's job very difficult and although Barbara Wright handled this task admirably and creatively, I was left wishing my French was good enough to read the original. It is much easier to read than Joyce and the tone is much lighter - one senses Queneau, like his heroine Zazie, saw life as a game in which nothing matters very much.<br /><br />I did enjoy it but overall felt it lacked substance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9193063761938763263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1615613111" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1615613111?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/24389390-duane">Duane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=2017-book-challenge">2017-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1615613111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9182911716803759534">"Zazie" was written by French writer Raymond Queneau (1903-1976) and published in 1959. It's well known for it's use of language. A sort of slang known as colloquial or Neo-French. For example, the words "who is it" are written "whozit". The book is loaded with this type of language, later referred to in France as "Zazie Speak". But to the average reader it's a farce. The escapades of a pre-teen girl (Zazie) while visiting her uncle (unkoo) Gabriel in Paris for the weekend. Her "unkoo" is a fema</span>
  <span id="freeText9182911716803759534" style="display:none">"Zazie" was written by French writer Raymond Queneau (1903-1976) and published in 1959. It's well known for it's use of language. A sort of slang known as colloquial or Neo-French. For example, the words "who is it" are written "whozit". The book is loaded with this type of language, later referred to in France as "Zazie Speak". But to the average reader it's a farce. The escapades of a pre-teen girl (Zazie) while visiting her uncle (unkoo) Gabriel in Paris for the weekend. Her "unkoo" is a female impersonator and this lends itself to many of the comic situations found in the book. <br /><br />The title is a little misleading because it gives the impression that Zazie spends all her time on the Metro, which is what she really wanted to see in Paris. But the Metro was on strike and Zazie never set foot on it that I remember. If you are looking for a change of pace, some comic relief, then I recommend getting acquainted with Zazie. It's laugh out loud funny in spots. 4.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="9182911716803759534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44610947" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44610947" />
    <a title="C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1425694-c"><img alt="C." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245484320p2/1425694.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44610947?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C." class="user" itemprop="url" name="C." href="/user/show/1425694-c">C.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">French speakers of all levels; the grammar is simple and you can guess the slang</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to C. by: </span>
      Manny et Michelle
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=i-wish-i-own">i-wish-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=to-be-re-read">to-be-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1425694-c?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44610947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9979303838650268161">I finally got around to finishing this, and it was a whole lot of fun. <br/><br/>1. For its linguistic ingenuity<br/>a) With respect to its highly accurate (I assume) transcription of Parisian slang<br/> - That brought back memories and a serious case of itchy feet<br/>b) With respect to the wonderful wordplay in which he engaged<br/> - I'm sure I missed most of this because my French isn't good enough<br/><br/>2. For its characters; they are, as it were, pretty great.<br/>a) With respect to the main players, who were all beautifully portr</span>
  <span id="freeText9979303838650268161" style="display:none">I finally got around to finishing this, and it was a whole lot of fun. <br /><br />1. For its linguistic ingenuity<br />a) With respect to its highly accurate (I assume) transcription of Parisian slang<br /> - That brought back memories and a serious case of itchy feet<br />b) With respect to the wonderful wordplay in which he engaged<br /> - I'm sure I missed most of this because my French isn't good enough<br /><br />2. For its characters; they are, as it were, pretty great.<br />a) With respect to the main players, who were all beautifully portrayed, highly colourful and very funny<br /> - Tonton Gabriel and Zazie and Marceline and Turandot (tu causes, tu causes...) and Madeleine and Trouscaillon and la Veuve Mouaque = JE VOUS AIME<br />b) With respect to the generally hilarious minor cameos<br /> - The rather wonderful tourists<br /><br />3. For its plot<br />a) With respect primarily to the fact that most of the characters end up discussing how to correctly conjugate a French verb, such as, for example, a woman with a man who has just stated his intention to rape her<br /> - Though not mentioned in this text (missed opportunity, Mr Queneau) the verb 'tapisser' (to cover, sprinkle with thickly, carpet, etc. etc.; presumably related to 'tapir' [rug:]?) is an annoying one - if you encounter it conjugated in the plural (i.e. nous, vous, ils/elles) it looks as if it is a regular -ir verb 'tapir', when in fact it is a regular -er verb. This I call a mean trick.<br /> - This is why French kids are still learning grammar in year 12<br /> - Because Australian kids never really learn grammar is why most of us suck at it<br />b) With respect secondarily to the fact that although it's very funny, it's also serious and people die (**SPOILER**: Sirius Black, in book 5) and it's actually rather sad<br /> - *tear*<br /><br />3. Clearly this is enough to recommend this book<br />a) Though I suspect it must be read in French<br /> - However I have read highly positive reviews from people who clearly read it in English (geste) so maybe a good translation exists for the francophonically-challenged<br /> - Incidentally, watching Futurama with French (or, I suppose, any other language) subtitles is hilarious because they translate all the jokes in the most literal way possible. Probably not recommended if you speak [insert LOTE here:] but not English. Though I would try this if it were possible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9979303838650268161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44610061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44610061" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44610061?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070333073" href="/book/show/913045.Zazie_dans_le_m_tro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=well-i-think-its-funny">well-i-think-its-funny</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44610061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4440312671996433034">This book is so funny!!! A perfect antidote to the Hamsun I read immediately before. Though I must say that it seriously tested the limits of my colloquial French... I thought I wasn't too bad at French slang, but this book put me firmly in my place. My edition appeared to be intended for French high school students, and there were many useful footnotes explaining the less obvious pieces of <i>argot</i>, obscure references, and neologisms (Queneau loves making up words). He also has an endearing way of</span>
  <span id="freeText4440312671996433034" style="display:none">This book is so funny!!! A perfect antidote to the Hamsun I read immediately before. Though I must say that it seriously tested the limits of my colloquial French... I thought I wasn't too bad at French slang, but this book put me firmly in my place. My edition appeared to be intended for French high school students, and there were many useful footnotes explaining the less obvious pieces of <i>argot</i>, obscure references, and neologisms (Queneau loves making up words). He also has an endearing way of spelling things phonetically for comic effect... you have to read everything aloud to yourself to figure it out. <br /><br />Given that I'm pretty sure I missed a lot of the humor, it is impressive that I still found myself laughing out loud every three or four pages. A native French speaker would probably have needed oxygen at a few points. The running joke with the parrot for some reason just gets better as the book progresses. 80% of Laverdure's lines are repetitions of "Tu cause, tu cause, c'est tout ce que tu sais faire", but after a while I cracked up every time he said it. I searched the phrase just now on Google, and some clever person has figured out that there are 19 chapters, and the parrot says it 19 times. I'm sure I could read this book once a year for the rest of my life, and still find at least a couple of new Easter eggs on each re-reading.<br /><br />Here was the passage I enjoyed most. (By the way, apropos comments on French slang above, it's usually much more challenging than this). Nice M. Turandot has foolishly tried to stop Zazie from going out on her own:<br /><br />Zazie n'hésite pas. Elle se mit à hurler<br /><br />- Au secours! Au secours!<br /><br />Ce cri ne manque pas d'attirer l'attention de ménagères et de citoyens présents. Ils abondonnent leurs occupations ou inoccupations personnelles pour s'intéresser à l'incident.<br />Après ce premier résultat assez satisfaisant, Zazie en remet:<br /><br />- Je veux pas aller avec le meussieu, je le connais pas le meussieu, je veux aller pas avec le meussieu.<br /><br />Exétéra.<br />Turandot, sûr de la noblesse de sa cause, fait fi de ces proférations. Il s'aperçoit bien vite qu'il a eu tort en constatant qu'il se trouve au centre d'un cercle de moralistes sévères.<br />Devant ce publie de choix, Zazie passe de considérations générales aux accusations particulières, précises et circonstanciées.<br /><br />- Ce meussieu, qu'elle dit comme ça, il m'a dit des choses sales.<br /><br />- Qu'est-ce qu'il t'a dit? demande une dame alléchée.<br /><br />- Madame! s'écrie Turandot, cette petite fille s'est sauvée de chez elle. Je la ramenais à ses parents.<br /><br />Le cercle ricane avec un scepticisme déjà solidement encré.<br />La dame insiste; elle se penche vers Zazie.<br /><br />- Allons, ma petite, n'aie pas peur, dis-le-moi ce qu'il t'a dit le villain meussieu?<br /><br />- C'est trop sale, murmure Zazie.<br /><br />- Il t'a demandé de lui faire des choses?<br /><br />- C'est ça, mdame.<br /><br />Zazie glisse à voix basse quelques détails dans l'oreille de la bonne femme. Celle-ci se redresse et crache à la figure de Turandot.<br /><br />- Dégueulasse, qu'elle lui jette en plus en prime.<br /><br />Et elle lui recrache une second fois de nouveau dessus, en plein poire.<br /><br />Un type s'enquiert:<br /><br />- Qu'est-ce qu'il lui a demandé de lui faire?<br /><br />La bonne femme glisse les détails zazique dans l'oreille du type:<br /><br />- Oh! qu'il fait le type, jamais j'avais pensé à ça.<br /><br />Il refait comme ça, plûtot pensivement:<br /><br />- Non, jamais.<br /><br />Il se tourne vers un autre citoyen:<br /><br />- Non mais, écoutez-moi ça... (détails). C'est pas croyab.<br /><br />- Ya vraiment des salauds complets, dit l'autre citoyen.<br /><br />Cependant, les details se propagent dans la foule. Une femme dit:<br /><br />- Comprends pas.<br /><br />Un homme lui explique. Il sort un bout de papier de sa poche et lui faire un dessin avec un stylo à bille.<br /><br />- Eh bien, dit la femme rêveusement.<br /><br />Elle ajoute:<br /><br />- Et c'est pratique?<br /><br />Elle parle du stylo à bille.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4440312671996433034" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_301063430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/301063430" />
    <a title="Geoff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126266-geoff"><img alt="Geoff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399505224p2/126266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/301063430?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoff" href="/user/show/126266-geoff">Geoff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126266-geoff?shelf=queneau">queneau</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer301063430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17466368108061749387">Q. Besides a lifespan that encompassed the first years of the 20th century through the late 1970’s, first-hand knowledge of the consequences of both world wars, a lifelong devotion to both wife and literature, a large body of work that spanned many genres, with a love of wordplay, humor, high diction, multilinguistic prose, a great attention to structure and experiments in form, and late recognition as one of the most important and influential writers in their respective language(s), what did Ra</span>
  <span id="freeText17466368108061749387" style="display:none">Q. Besides a lifespan that encompassed the first years of the 20th century through the late 1970’s, first-hand knowledge of the consequences of both world wars, a lifelong devotion to both wife and literature, a large body of work that spanned many genres, with a love of wordplay, humor, high diction, multilinguistic prose, a great attention to structure and experiments in form, and late recognition as one of the most important and influential writers in their respective language(s), what did Raymond Queneau and Vladimir Vladimirovich Nabokov have in common?<br /><br />A. They both wrote popular novels about nymphish, foul-mouthed, just-pubescent girls that were made into successful films by respected directors, and which made their names and fortunes, and which consequently overshadowed other equally or superiorly deserving work in their oeuvre, to the point that when each passed away (one in ‘76, the other in ‘77), a large number of obituaries placed the names of their fictional creations, their girl-child counterparts, adjacent their own names in large-font headlines reporting said deaths.<br /><br />I really don’t need to write a review of <i>Zazie</i>, do I? It’s a certified classic for chrissakes! Penguin says so. It’s everything Queneau does amplified, refined, distilled, and... <i>crystallized</i>?! Crystallized my arse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17466368108061749387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_78067201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78067201" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78067201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16993450409759936583">This short whimsical novel from the Parisian polymath (and co-founder of the Oulipo) isn’t representative of his phenomenal talent, but is a tittersome romp through a cinematic Paris of the 1950s with the acid-tongued Zazie the charming misfit at its core.<br/><br/>The humour was, for its time, subversive, with its foul-mouthed heroine, the consistent references to ‘homossesuality’ and the playfully childish words spelled phonetically throughout the text. There is no plot as such, minus Zazie’s persistent</span>
  <span id="freeText16993450409759936583" style="display:none">This short whimsical novel from the Parisian polymath (and co-founder of the Oulipo) isn’t representative of his phenomenal talent, but is a tittersome romp through a cinematic Paris of the 1950s with the acid-tongued Zazie the charming misfit at its core.<br /><br />The humour was, for its time, subversive, with its foul-mouthed heroine, the consistent references to ‘homossesuality’ and the playfully childish words spelled phonetically throughout the text. There is no plot as such, minus Zazie’s persistent dissatisfaction at being unable to ride the metro, but Queneau uses witty dialogue and crackling comic prose to keep us entertained.<br /><br />This novel rightfully takes its place in the canon of classic comic works with the efforts of Wodehouse, Kingsley Amis and Douggie Adams, and has been adapted into a cult French film and a comic strip. So there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16993450409759936583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3537273154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3537273154" />
    <a title="Christian Doig" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20697503-christian-doig"><img alt="Christian Doig" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571371857p2/20697503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3537273154?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christian Doig" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christian Doig" href="/user/show/20697503-christian-doig">Christian Doig</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/24806303-zazie">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">aficionados a la literatura lúdica, francófilos</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20697503-christian-doig?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3537273154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18211576566557438123">Zazie dans le métro is an absurdist, comedic and ultimately surrealistic novel that has striken me as almost nothing I was expecting it to be --even though its equally classic film counterpart nurtured my privileged childhood (of course).<br/><br/>Ever-quirky, but with a subtly increasing ominous streak about it, Queneau's book is a subversive fiction, a rarefied and peculiar, parodic coming-of-age story depicting a Paris of homeless people, deceiving appearances, hopeless aspirations and "sex fiends" out</span>
  <span id="freeText18211576566557438123" style="display:none">Zazie dans le métro is an absurdist, comedic and ultimately surrealistic novel that has striken me as almost nothing I was expecting it to be --even though its equally classic film counterpart nurtured my privileged childhood (of course).<br /><br />Ever-quirky, but with a subtly increasing ominous streak about it, Queneau's book is a subversive fiction, a rarefied and peculiar, parodic coming-of-age story depicting a Paris of homeless people, deceiving appearances, hopeless aspirations and "sex fiends" out to get our little protagonist, a 11-year-old or fully pubescent girl --a circumstance that is left intentionally vague for the reader to ponder on and that makes the tendency of the male adults around to think of her as a Lolita or a streetwalker somehow more disturbing-- with a sharp tongue underlain by a shocking maturity that speaks of some very dark corruption, one that goes even beyond the child's startling backstory of sexual abuse and domestic murder. The novel is a self-conscious play and its heroine a paradoxical symbol of human cynicism: "[Insert any given word here] my ass" is her motto. Irresistibly funny yet profoundly sad at once.<br /><br />In the end, Zazie dans le métro kind of takes off its mask of intellectual caricature by revealing the melancholy of growing up in a spiritually confused society. Like Salinger, Queneau too seems to hold adulthood as the moral abyss of existence, and Zazie emerges from the light design of this dubious milieu, if not unscathed, wiser and most probably more foul-mouthed than she ever was.<br /><br />"Grownups my ass."</span>
  <a data-text-id="18211576566557438123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/679730604" />
    <a title="J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1126891-j"><img alt="J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329622684p2/1126891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J." href="/user/show/1126891-j">J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer679730604" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1267459944028603986">A fizzy, surreal-adjacent rendition of what might in the parlance of the sixties been called a 'romp through the low-lights' of postwar Paris. <br/><br/>Tweenish, precocious pre-ingénue Zazie, and everyone in her trail/path engage in exactly the wrong sort of thing to be doing for 1959 France. <br/><br/>In 2014 this would be best on a beach with a cool <i>Fernet Branca</i>, an afternoon's read that doesn't last longer than the fizz. Some of the outrageousness-- lots of drinking, some cage-aux-folles crossdress cabaret, in</span>
  <span id="freeText1267459944028603986" style="display:none">A fizzy, surreal-adjacent rendition of what might in the parlance of the sixties been called a 'romp through the low-lights' of postwar Paris. <br /><br />Tweenish, precocious pre-ingénue Zazie, and everyone in her trail/path engage in exactly the wrong sort of thing to be doing for 1959 France. <br /><br />In 2014 this would be best on a beach with a cool <i>Fernet Branca</i>, an afternoon's read that doesn't last longer than the fizz. Some of the outrageousness-- lots of drinking, some cage-aux-folles crossdress cabaret, insistently constant irreverence --would have been hilarious in '59, but now feel a bit more like a time capsule.<br /><br />The very good introduction by Gilbert Adair points out the similarities in pace, and in tone and content, to the films of the French New Wave, also an irreverent postwar phenom, and the comparison is apt.<br /><br />Best to save this one for a nice railroad trip or plane ride to a holiday locale; there is room in the world for lightweight and breezy, and you have it right here.<br /><br />"Lightweight and breezy my arse," the protagonist --protagonette?-- would certainly say. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1267459944028603986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2870411218" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2870411218" />
    <a title="Doug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25667717-doug"><img alt="Doug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605117426p2/25667717.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2870411218?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug" href="/user/show/25667717-doug">Doug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2870411218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10770033019437910662">Not sure how I made it to my ripe old age with never having read Queneau's best known work, nor viewing Malle's iconic film made from the same ... but both were a mite disappointing - although this IS one of the rare cases when the film MIGHT be slightly better than the book. At least, I enjoyed Malle's visuals more than Queneau's verse, which even in such a short work got repetitious and tedious - and often incomprehensible. More clever than actually witty or LOL funny, Queneau's verbal pyrotec</span>
  <span id="freeText10770033019437910662" style="display:none">Not sure how I made it to my ripe old age with never having read Queneau's best known work, nor viewing Malle's iconic film made from the same ... but both were a mite disappointing - although this IS one of the rare cases when the film MIGHT be slightly better than the book. At least, I enjoyed Malle's visuals more than Queneau's verse, which even in such a short work got repetitious and tedious - and often incomprehensible. More clever than actually witty or LOL funny, Queneau's verbal pyrotechnics have been likened to Joyce, but I would say his anarchistic Punch &amp; Judy-ish antics bear a much clearer debt to Alfred Jarry. And though I take all of Zazie's incessant questioning over Uncle Gabriel's alleged 'hormosessuality' (sic) as more or less a sign of the time in which it was written, it still slightly grates. Glad I finally read it, but have a feeling it will all be a blur within a week.<br /><br />PS ... I can see how much the film was a direct influence on Jeunet in his "Amelie'... </span>
  <a data-text-id="10770033019437910662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3199120920" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3199120920" />
    <a title="Vishy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5555413-vishy"><img alt="Vishy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307126420p2/5555413.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3199120920?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vishy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vishy" href="/user/show/5555413-vishy">Vishy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3199120920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12065586113940598132">When I was trying to explore French literature a few years back, one of my friends recommended Raymond Queneau's 'Zazie in the Metro'. I couldn't read it at that time, but finally got to read it today. <br/><br/>Zazie arrives at Paris from the countryside where she lives. Her mother hands her over to her uncle for the next two days. Zazie has one dream that she wants to realize during her time in Paris. She wants to travel by the metro. But the metro is not operational that day because the metro employees</span>
  <span id="freeText12065586113940598132" style="display:none">When I was trying to explore French literature a few years back, one of my friends recommended Raymond Queneau's 'Zazie in the Metro'. I couldn't read it at that time, but finally got to read it today. <br /><br />Zazie arrives at Paris from the countryside where she lives. Her mother hands her over to her uncle for the next two days. Zazie has one dream that she wants to realize during her time in Paris. She wants to travel by the metro. But the metro is not operational that day because the metro employees are on strike. Zazie is not interested in anything else and is bored at her uncle's home. Then she decides to step out of her uncle's home and wander the streets and meets a stranger and one thing leads to another, and before she knows she is involved in one adventure after another. I thought the book would be about Zazie's adventures, but at some point the cast of characters in the book expands, the action explodes, things get crazy, there are surprises revealed, and before long the story resembles a screwball comedy. <br /><br />Raymond Queneau's prose has a lot of wordplay and it is very enjoyable to read. For example, this sentence :<br /><br />"'Coming!' she replied, just loudly enough to enable her words to cleave the air with the desired speed and intensity."<br /><br />And this one :<br /><br />"...analysing this strange behaviour, some according to deductive reasoning, others according to inductive..."<br /><br />And this one :<br /><br />"...whose nascent passion had not entirely obnubilated her native cartesianism"<br /><br />And this one : <br /><br />"At the hour when one is wont to drink soft drinks of strong colour and strong drinks of pale colour..."<br /><br />And this one, which made me smile :)<br /><br />"Zazie has joined Laverdure in somnia."<br /><br />Wordplay is very difficult to translate from one language to another, especially between two languages which have very different grammatical structures like French and English. The English translation of the wordplay is enjoyable, but one can't help wondering whether it is an accurate translation (and whether such a thing is possible) or an acceptable anglicized one, and how much more beautiful the French version must be. Zazie and many other characters also seem to speak in a regional dialect of French and that contributes a lot to the atmosphere, the style, the mood and the humour in the story. The translator has attempted the impossible task of rendering this in English, and one can't stop admiring her heroic effort, but it is also hard to avoid the sneaky feeling of despair that this is a fool's errand.<br /><br />A few times, Queneau sneaks in some philosophical passages, without revealing his intentions – we are not sure whether we should contemplate seriously on them or whether we should laugh aloud because of the underlying, understated humour :) One of my favourites of these philosophical passages was this one : <br /><br />"Being or nothingness, that is the question. Ascending, descending, coming, going, a man does so much that in the end he disappears. A taxi bears him off, a metro carries him away, the Tower doesn't care, nor the Panthéon. Paris is but a dream, Gabriel is but a reverie (a charming one), Zazie the dream of a reverie (or of a nightmare) and all this story the dream of a dream, the reverie of a reverie, scarcely more than the typewritten delirium of an idiotic novelist."<br /><br />'Zazie in the Metro' is a rip-roaring, irreverent comedy from the beginning to the end with wonderful wordplay. Zazie, with her sharp wit and repartee, wisdom beyond her age, and the ability to annoy grown-ups effortlessly with her intelligent retorts and quick wordplay, is one of the cool, stylish characters in fiction. 'Zazie in the Metro' is probably very different from other Raymond Queneau books, because the Raymond Queneau who wrote this book was not the nerd Queneau, the 'intellectual and polymath of the highest order' who founded the Oulipo group. The Raymond Queneau who wrote this book was a person who loved fun, who loved humour, who loved wordplay, and who revealed his boyish, fun side to us through this book. I enjoyed very much seeing this side of Raymond Queneau. This book was adapted into a film too, and it was well-received when it first came out. I want to watch that sometime.<br /><br />Have you read 'Zazie in the Metro'? What do you think about it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="12065586113940598132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169122143" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169122143" />
    <a title="Maggie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4222084-maggie"><img alt="Maggie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319666686p2/4222084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169122143?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maggie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maggie" href="/user/show/4222084-maggie">Maggie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=a-z-book-2011">a-z-book-2011</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=french-authors">french-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169122143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15190267709109795640">Zazie Lalochère is my hero, or perhaps antihero. Both? She's a preteen-teen (her age is never stated) from the French country who gets dropped off with her uncle, Gabriel, in Paris for two days so her mother can spend time with her new boyfriend. Immediately, it's obvious that Zazie is a character. When she finds out that the metro she so desperately wants to ride is closed due to a strike, she cries, "Oo the bastards!" But the moment she wins my heart comes a few pages later when she declares s</span>
  <span id="freeText15190267709109795640" style="display:none">Zazie Lalochère is my hero, or perhaps antihero. Both? She's a preteen-teen (her age is never stated) from the French country who gets dropped off with her uncle, Gabriel, in Paris for two days so her mother can spend time with her new boyfriend. Immediately, it's obvious that Zazie is a character. When she finds out that the metro she so desperately wants to ride is closed due to a strike, she cries, "Oo the bastards!" But the moment she wins my heart comes a few pages later when she declares she wants to be a teacher. Her aunt and uncle are suitably impressed and say teaching is a good profession with a good pension. Zazie responds, "Pension my arse." The reason she wants to be a teacher is actually: "To bitch up the brats." <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380350054i/703965.jpg" width="287" height="460" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Everyone in this book, from Zazie to Gabriel to the landlord's parrot, is actually kind of an asshole. The landlord calls Zazie a little slut, Zazie spits in his face and accuses him of being a pedophile in front of a crowd of people. Hijinks ensue. But as the great Ms. Clairee Belcher once said:<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380350054ra/703966.gif" width="500" height="222" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The book is really just a series of random scenes punctuated by smartass dialogue and the occasional man-napping and attempted rape.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380350054i/703967.jpg" width="500" height="291" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The dialogue might also be annoying at first because a lot of it is written colloquially.<br><blockquote>"I'll ksplain," says Gabriel.</blockquote>Usually went someone sends me a text like that ("Wot r u doin 2day?"), I want to kill myself. However, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau" title="Raymond Queneau" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond Queneau</a> isn't some undereducated teenager. As explained in the Introduction, this was a linguistic experiment and parody by the man who co-founded the Ouvroir de Littérature Potentielle, "a literary workshop whose raison d'être was the invention of fiendish linguistic constraints." Basically, Queneau was having some fun with <i>Zazie</i>. And that's why, despite the seeming randomness of plot and phonetic dialogue, <i>Zazie in the Metro</i> works for me. It's not meant to be highbrow, though it's written by someone in the highest echelons of French intelligentsia, and it doesn't seem like it wants to <i>be</i> anything. Yet, thanks to Zazie, it's still memorable and entertaining. It just feels very French. That may not be a strong enough reason to read this for a lot of people, but it was more than enough for me and I thoroughly enjoyed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15190267709109795640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_30292291" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30292291" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30292291?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30292291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14360884971602714166">To truly appreciate Queneau’s wonderfully irreverent masterpiece, ‘Zazie in the Metro’, the reader must envelop themselves within the wonderful world of popular culture, must embrace the platitudinous dialogue, cardboard characters and hackneyed scenarios of pulp fiction; Queneau, like an alchemist, swallows the conventions of popular culture and regurgitates them in the form of Zazie; the impious and impish heroine of the novel, foul-mouthed and flippant, Zazie, like all of the characters withi</span>
  <span id="freeText14360884971602714166" style="display:none">To truly appreciate Queneau’s wonderfully irreverent masterpiece, ‘Zazie in the Metro’, the reader must envelop themselves within the wonderful world of popular culture, must embrace the platitudinous dialogue, cardboard characters and hackneyed scenarios of pulp fiction; Queneau, like an alchemist, swallows the conventions of popular culture and regurgitates them in the form of Zazie; the impious and impish heroine of the novel, foul-mouthed and flippant, Zazie, like all of the characters within the book has no more dimensions or depth, but that is the point-Queneau is exploring the nature of popular fiction, is upending and at the same time paying homage to its tropes. <br /><br />The cast of secondary characters, from the perpetually blushing Marceline, to the cross-dressing ballet dancer Gabriel or the oafish yet sinister Trouscaillon of the many aliases, all of these are the mannequins who Queneau dresses with the tropes of popular fiction-from the shoot-out, to the chase scene, to the slapsticks fight scenes, Queneau lampoons and explores the artifice of pulp fiction. In many ways, his highly stylized novels prefigure the films of the French New Wave, who were also deeply influenced by popular culture and, like Queneau aimed to transcend its commonplace nature and transform it into something magical. Whilst not laugh-out-loud funny, ‘Zazie’ is full of humour and puns-some of which may be lost in translation, however the puns and double entendres of French slang are brilliantly translated by Barbara Wright. Although ‘Zazie’ sits just below ‘Exercises in Style’ as one of the masterpieces of French literature, it is nonetheless a good introduction to the least pretentious of all the great French writers, Raymond Queneau and Louis Malle’s film version is also wonderful. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14360884971602714166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7189864387671762763" style="display:none">A young country girl, Zazie, comes to Paris for a few days, trusted into the rather dodgy care of her Uncle Gabriel, but the city proves something of a disappointment -most of all she wants to ride the metro, but it’s workers have gone on strike. <br />Zazie, at Id guess 10 years old, has an appealing mix of preciousness and innocence which plays well against her uncle’s drag act in a dubious nightclub. <br />It’s an entertaining and quick read that’s really good fun, a cross-section of Parisian 1960s society examined under Zazie's ruthless gaze...<br />...but, with a lot of dialogue, much of it in slang, I’m going hazard a guess that it’s been a thankless task to translate (<i>Barbara Wright</i>); one of those books that despite her making it very readable, it can’t possibly do the original proper justice. </span>

  <span id="freeText17452139183748939639" style="display:none">Leaving aside the fact that this kind of literature is completely incomprehensible for people like me who like their books with a semblance of a plot and a logical dialogue, I cannot help to think that sadly my grasp of French is too basic for me to read the book in the original, which would have mightily improved the chance of me finding the book intriguing. This seems to be one of those books best enjoyed in their initial language by people who can appreciate the word-forging ingenuity of the author. I cannot judge the quality of the translation, but in fact it only managed to give me the sense that I'm missing a lot. DNF at 77%.</span>

<div id="review_318898127" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/318898127" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/318898127?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1959">books-from-1959</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=literature-in-translation">literature-in-translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer318898127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6965396463757026676">It's hard to remember a time before I could go to Google and look up anything I wanted to know. In early 2007 I was planning a trip to Paris and though I'm sure I was using search engines by then, my searching skills were not up to creating a reading list of contemporary novels set in Paris. I had recently begun reading a blog called The Millions, one of the few literary blogs I still check daily. They have a feature called "Ask A Book Question," so I did!<br/> <br/>The first recommendation I got in the a</span>
  <span id="freeText6965396463757026676" style="display:none">It's hard to remember a time before I could go to Google and look up anything I wanted to know. In early 2007 I was planning a trip to Paris and though I'm sure I was using search engines by then, my searching skills were not up to creating a reading list of contemporary novels set in Paris. I had recently begun reading a blog called The Millions, one of the few literary blogs I still check daily. They have a feature called "Ask A Book Question," so I did!<br /> <br />The first recommendation I got in the answer was Zazie in the Metro. As it turned out, I only read one book set in Paris before I went. (Black Girl in Paris, by Shay Youngblood and it was excellent.) But one day during my stay, I braved the Metro by myself and made it to the famous Shakespeare and Company Bookstore. There I found and bought Zazie in the Metro. I even got the store's rubber stamp logo on the first page as well as a cool bookmark that I use to this day.<br /><br />But I still did not read the book. It was originally published in France in 1959, so got slotted into My Big Fat Reading Project list for that year. Currently I am about two-thirds of the way through my 1959 reading list and finally have read Zazie.<br /><br />It's all good because had I read it five years ago, I doubt that I'd have known what I was reading. Now I have heard of Raymond Queneau, since he was a well-known executive at Gallimard and a friend of Jean Paul Sartre and Simone de Beauvoir. Queneau was also the founder of OULIPO (a group of experimental writers) about which I know only a tiny bit but at least I have heard of it.<br /><br />So in the wondrous way that reading takes me through ideas and characters and places, I arrived on the first page of Zazie in the Metro to read the first word: "Howcanaystinksotho." The book is a tongue-in-cheek, play-on-words romp through Paris and the French slang of the 1950s. Intrepidly translated into English by Barbara Wright, those of us whose French is slight can enjoy the fun.<br /><br />Zazie's single mom has dropped her daughter off in Paris for a weekend. While Mom spends the time with a boyfriend, Zazie is supposed to be looked after by her female impersonator of an uncle. She never gets to ride the Metro, though it is her heart's desire, because the transportation workers are on strike. Being on the cusp of her teenage years, having zero manners and even less fear of anything, she does manage to score a pair of jeans as she falls in with all sorts of quirky and sometimes unsavory characters.<br /><br />Even so, it took me a good half of the book to get my bearings. By the end I was laughing at the humor, getting some of the word play, and had figured out the characters, one of whom is a hilarious parrot. Queneau was a serious intellectual who had already written twelve novels before this one, but like Nabokov with his prepubescent heroine Lolita, Zazie brought her author to mainstream readers. The novel was even made as a movie in 1960.<br /><br />Spoof and satire are not my usual reading preference, but I was entertained. I saw Paris from yet another perspective. I must figure out my next trip to Paris.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6965396463757026676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_1332156295" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1332156295" />
    <a title="Roz  Milner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3104186-roz-milner"><img alt="Roz  Milner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546430226p2/3104186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1332156295?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roz  Milner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roz  Milner" href="/user/show/3104186-roz-milner">Roz  Milner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104186-roz-milner?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104186-roz-milner?shelf=fiction-i-d-recommend">fiction-i-d-recommend</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104186-roz-milner?shelf=world-lit">world-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1332156295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6506521886427696565">Sometimes, I’ll call a book or story cinematic. Normally, I mean it as a way to say the writing is particularly visual, like the words immediately lend themselves to being seen. For example, when I thin of Casey Plett’s story “Not Bleak” (see: A Safe Girl to Love), I can picture the wide-open fields of the Canadian praries and the small Mennonite community, not to mention Zeke withdrawing into herself as they cross the border. <br/><br/>But here’s something else that seems cinematic, in a different sense </span>
  <span id="freeText6506521886427696565" style="display:none">Sometimes, I’ll call a book or story cinematic. Normally, I mean it as a way to say the writing is particularly visual, like the words immediately lend themselves to being seen. For example, when I thin of Casey Plett’s story “Not Bleak” (see: A Safe Girl to Love), I can picture the wide-open fields of the Canadian praries and the small Mennonite community, not to mention Zeke withdrawing into herself as they cross the border. <br /><br />But here’s something else that seems cinematic, in a different sense of the word. Zazie at the Metro is cinematic in a Buster Keaton sense; it’s essentially the story of a young girl visiting her uncle in the city and wanting to ride the subway. But it goes off the rails in a series of increasingly madcap adventures, witty wordplay and punning, and alcohol. As written, the book practically is a screenplay; small wonder it was made into a film only a short while later.<br /><br />As noted, it’s about a young kid visiting her uncle. That’s Zazie, visiting Paris to see her uncle Gabriel. She doesn’t like him, or anybody else, and is fixated on riding the Metro. Which is closed up because of a strike. Instead, Zazie starts amusing herself, stealing, lying and running amok through Paris. She – with Gabriel, his friend Charles and a handful of accomplices in tow – get mixed up in police conspiracies, different varieties of kidnapping and a drag nightclub. <br /><br />It sounds simple enough, right? And it’s a wild story, the kind that gets crazier as it goes on, kind of like one of those old Keaton or Harold Lloyd flicks, where the hero ends up chased through town by thousands of people or climbs a skyscraper with their bare hands. The fun is in the telling, not in the realism. <br /><br />Which gets me to my favourite thing about this book: the telling. During a life as a public intellectual, Queneau was known as something of a lingual jokester. One of his books is Exercises in Style, where a simple story is told in different forms, over and over, with Queneau parodying everything and anything. This is similar. <br /><br />Throughout, he writes characters as a collection of clichés and accents, their words running together and slipping into puns. Zazie goes from childlike innocence to asking existential questions in a moment, characters emptily rely with “(gesture)” or “(sigh)”, and Queneau reaches for the wordiest of saying things: “the cameras crepitated,” and tourists talk with “a great berlitzscoulian effort.”<br /><br />Or, for example, here’s a small taste of Queneau’s prose:<br /><br />“Gabriel’s admirers had already installed him comfortably and, equipped with adequate apparatuses, were measuring the weight of the light in order to take his portrait with silhouette effect…” (pg 76)<br /><br />The Penguin Classics edition is nice, coming with a decent introduction by Gilbert Adair, who sets the stage for the book: he sketches out Queneau’s biography, explains the reactions to this little book and draws a comparison between it and French New Wave, particularly to the films of Godard. <br /><br />The translation by Barbara Wright is good, too. Between all the puns and linguistic wordplay, I imagine the text was a pain to translate. Although I found it a tad British at times – the police arrive in a black maria, for example – I can’t really speak to that being something she added or if it’s more a reflection of a particularly French synonym that defies translation. It certainly makes me want to polish up my abysmal French skills, anyway.<br /><br />All in all, a wild, silly and highly enjoyable ride. If you’re the kind of person who likes re-reading stuff, there’s plenty of wordplay and such to go back and chew on; if you’re more into reading comic adventures, there’s a lot of that too. It’s probably a tad too clever for some people – I can imagine some saying it strays too far from reality for their tastes – but still, I’d recommend it unreservedly. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6506521886427696565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
