<div id="review_336961437" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/336961437" />
    <a title="Rozzer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8534793-rozzer"><img alt="Rozzer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339183483p2/8534793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/336961437?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rozzer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rozzer" href="/user/show/8534793-rozzer">Rozzer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070364022" href="/book/show/6234465-zazie-dans-le-m-tro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8534793-rozzer?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8534793-rozzer?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8534793-rozzer?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8534793-rozzer?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer336961437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17162306379040946754">Spring of 1962. A junior in high school. Had seen and read the "What is 'Pataphysics" issue of the Evergreen Review the prior year. So when I saw that the movie Zazie, after the book by Queneau, was playing at the Paris near the Plaza I asked Shelley to be my date and see the movie. To which she agreed and we went. I loved it. The whole craziness of Zazie, her uncle and his household. This was (to me) very exciting stuff. But then I was already at the time nuts for almost anything French. Which </span>
  <span id="freeText17162306379040946754" style="display:none">Spring of 1962. A junior in high school. Had seen and read the "What is 'Pataphysics" issue of the Evergreen Review the prior year. So when I saw that the movie Zazie, after the book by Queneau, was playing at the Paris near the Plaza I asked Shelley to be my date and see the movie. To which she agreed and we went. I loved it. The whole craziness of Zazie, her uncle and his household. This was (to me) very exciting stuff. But then I was already at the time nuts for almost anything French. Which didn't include dinner that evening. No. For dinner Shelley and I went to see her uncle on Eighth Avenue. Somewhere north of Madison Square Garden. Her uncle ran a fry shop, a kind of precursor to fast food. Not in the decor or maintenance, in the food. <br /><br />The place had obviously been built in the twenties or thirties and never touched since. Dingy. But (fantastic!) you could get any possible kind of fried food on a tray and then go sit in one of the old exam chairs in the back of the shop and eat it. (An exam chair has a writing surface in front that can be folded down to permit you easy entry to and egress from the seat.) For me, this was heaven. All the fried food we could eat for nothing because the boss (in his not-all-that-clean, big white apron behind the counter) was Shelley's uncle. A very, very Queaneau-esque personage and situation. As I was later to find out.<br /><br />Zazie. I liked the nutty manner in which the story "progresses" (though to say "progresses" is not particularly accurate). Instead of imposed chronological, deterministic exactitude (Toot! Toot! says the train), the story could and does branch out in all directions, including even sometimes doubling back on itself. This creates a very, very relaxed atmosphere of anything can happen, like driving a bumper-car at a fair. The whole story being driven, when needed, by the recurrent discovery on Zazie's part at various times during the film that the Metro was STILL on strike. <br /><br />It's nice that exploratory movies of this kind could be made and that they still can be made in our own day. In relative terms, their cost has decreased, because the blockbusters of today are so vastly more expensive than those in the past that exploratory films now cost less than they did before. The money people will be no more happy with such attempts than they ever have been, which means not very happy at all. But a million in production costs today is absolute peanuts, the kind of sum a good producer today can raise at his neighborhood coffee shop in two hours or so. <br /><br />Queneau is always going to be a minority taste, which is, of course, what he always wanted to be. Here's a novelist who pays the bills by being a bank executive, while at the same time (for example) giving lectures on reading Hegel. All his fiction very consciously relates to advanced mathematics, though a prospective reader doesn't have to worry about this because a reader can't ever find any mathematical allusions or expressions in the work itself. Both Queneau's work and his mindframe are quintessentially French, and any aspiring Francophile will sooner or later have to come to terms with Ray Queneau. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="17162306379040946754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2090947900?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=france">france</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2090947900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5051239496819030008">This is not a word I use in book reviews, but with this one, it's <i>de rigeur</i>: The word is <i>romp</i>. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28369.Zazie_in_the_Metro' title='Zazie in the Metro by Raymond Queneau' rel='nofollow noopener'>Zazie in the Metro</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau' title='Raymond Queneau' rel='nofollow noopener'>Raymond Queneau</a> is the tale of how a little girl from the provinces spends 36 hours in the City of Paris. She is frustrated in her main goal, which was to ride the Metro (i.e., the subway); but, alas, the Metro is on strike during her visit.<br/><br/>Being a member of Oulipo, a loose organization of French writers who enjoy using linguistic constraints of various sorts. <b>Zazie</b> is, in this se</span>
  <span id="freeText5051239496819030008" style="display:none">This is not a word I use in book reviews, but with this one, it's <i>de rigeur</i>: The word is <i>romp</i>. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28369.Zazie_in_the_Metro" title="Zazie in the Metro by Raymond Queneau" rel="nofollow noopener">Zazie in the Metro</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau" title="Raymond Queneau" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond Queneau</a> is the tale of how a little girl from the provinces spends 36 hours in the City of Paris. She is frustrated in her main goal, which was to ride the Metro (i.e., the subway); but, alas, the Metro is on strike during her visit.<br /><br />Being a member of Oulipo, a loose organization of French writers who enjoy using linguistic constraints of various sorts. <b>Zazie</b> is, in this sense, Oulipo Lite. The first word in the novel, in Barbara Wright's translation, is "Howcanaystinksotho." There are simply buckets of made-up words, most of which are fairly comprehensible.<br /><br />Zazie herself -- who, curiously, is never described in the novel -- is pretty much of a pistol. She is staring with her Uncle Gabriel, a transvestite entertainer. When she goes wandering off by herself in the morning, she is followed by a possible cop, possible sex offender (who turns out to be several other things besides). He buys her a pair of blue jeans and takes her back to her uncle.<br /><br />Louis Malle made a delightful film from this book, <b>Zazie dans le metro</b> (1961). </span>
  <a data-text-id="5051239496819030008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('s8gPR0Q6WrKV0G0WOYGJp25E3i4wQkc1IstiMn0E5f03CGiBqK3/4P/XtuP8TRvv0RpHxzUq/+V6pLpCV42Aog==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1pznHM2yJrtnTA+9TSIKjo990F0ObN5IE1F09uLlCcpSXIDaISWD6Q1L1EiI7pjGMCNJtAsEZphLPqyGyGxslQ==')})
  }

Dans ce monde où les pianos sont des mélangeurs à cocktails, la réalité semble ne pas avoir de prise. On se marie à l\'église comme on va à la fête foraine et on ignore le travail, qui se réduit à une usine monstrueuse faisant tache sur le paysage. Pied de nez aux conventions romanesques et à la morale commune, L\'Écume des jours est un délice verbal et un festin poétique. Jeux de mots, néologismes, décalages incongrus... Vian surenchérit sans cesse, faisant naître comme un vertige chez le lecteur hébété, qui sourit quand il peut. Mais le véritable malaise vient d\'ailleurs : ces adolescents éternels à la sensibilité exacerbée constituent des victimes de choix. L\'obsession consumériste de Chick, née d\'une idolâtrie frénétique pour un certain Jean-Sol Partre, semble vouloir dire que le bonheur ne saurait durer. En effet, l\'asphyxie gagne du terrain, et l\'on assiste avec effroi au rétrécissement inexorable des appartements.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau">About Raymond Queneau</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1491630480p3/15957.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/15957.Raymond_Queneau">Raymond Queneau
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
448
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8149411790896844324">Novelist, poet, and critic Raymond Queneau, was born in Le Havre in 1903, and went to Paris when he was 17. For some time he joined André Breton's Surrealist group, but after only a brief stint he dissociated himself. Now, seeing Queneau's work in retrospect, it seems inevitable. The Surrealists tried to achieve a sort of pure expression from the unconscious, without mediation of the author's self</span>
  <span id="freeText8149411790896844324" style="display:none">Novelist, poet, and critic Raymond Queneau, was born in Le Havre in 1903, and went to Paris when he was 17. For some time he joined André Breton's Surrealist group, but after only a brief stint he dissociated himself. Now, seeing Queneau's work in retrospect, it seems inevitable. The Surrealists tried to achieve a sort of pure expression from the unconscious, without mediation of the author's self-aware "persona." Queneau's texts, on the contrary, are quite deliberate products of the author's conscious mind, of his memory, and his intentionality.<br /><br />Although Queneau's novels give an impression of enormous spontaneity, they were in fact painstakingly conceived in every small detail. He even once remarked that he simply could not leave to hazard the task of determining the number of chapters of a book. Talking about his first novel, Le Chiendent (usually translated as The Bark Tree), he pointed out that it had 91 sections, because 91 was the sum of the first 13 numbers, and also the product of two numbers he was particularly fond of: 7 and 13.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8149411790896844324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/517194-zazie-dans-le-m-tro">Trivia About Zazie in the Metro</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/517194-zazie-dans-le-m-tro">Quotes from Zazie in the Metro</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Being or nothing, that is the question. Ascending, descending, coming, going, a man does so much that in the end he disappears.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/314051-being-or-nothing-that-is-the-question-ascending-descending-coming">63 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Why," he was saying, "why should one not tolerate this life, since so little suffices to deprive one of it? So little brings it into being, so little brightens it, so little blights it, so little bears it away. Otherwise, who would tolerate the blows of fate and the humiliations of a successful career, the swindling of grocers, the prices of butchers, the water of milkmen, the irritation of parents, the fury of teachers, the bawling of sergeant-majors, the turpitude of the beasts, the lamentations of the dead-beats, the silence of infinite space, the smell of cauliflower or the passivity of the wooden horses on a merry-g0-round, were it not for his knowledge that the bad and proliferative behaviour of certain minute cells (gesture) or the trajectory of a bullet traced by an involuntary, irresponsible, anonymous individual might unexpectedly come and cause all these cares to evaporate into the blue heavens.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1291718-why-he-was-saying-why-should-one-not-tolerate-this">4 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
